<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama_(disambiguation)" title="Vasco da Gama (disambiguation)">Vasco da Gama (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p><b>Vasco da Gama</b>, 1st <a href="/wiki/Count_of_Vidigueira" title="Count of Vidigueira">Count of Vidigueira</a> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">UK</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">US</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <span style="font-size:85%;">European Portuguese: </span><span class="IPA" lang="pt-PT-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Portuguese" title="Help:IPA/Portuguese">[ˈvaʃku ðɐ ˈɣɐ̃mɐ]</a></span>; c. 1460s – 24 December 1524), was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portugal_in_the_Age_of_Discovery" title="Portugal in the Age of Discovery">Portuguese explorer</a> and the first European to reach India by sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>His initial voyage to India by way of <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> (1497–1499) was the first to link Europe and Asia by an ocean route, connecting the Atlantic and the Indian oceans. This is widely considered a milestone in world history, as it marked the beginning of a sea-based phase of global <a href="/wiki/Multiculturalism" title="Multiculturalism">multiculturalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Da Gama's <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_discovery_of_the_sea_route_to_India" title="Portuguese discovery of the sea route to India">discovery of the sea route to India</a> opened the way for an age of global <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a> and enabled the Portuguese to establish a long-lasting <a href="/wiki/Colonial_empire" title="Colonial empire">colonial empire</a> along the way from Africa to Asia. The violence and hostage-taking employed by da Gama and those who followed also assigned a brutal reputation to the Portuguese among India's indigenous kingdoms that would set the pattern for <a href="/wiki/History_of_colonialism#Portuguese_and_Spanish_exploration_and_colonization" title="History of colonialism">western colonialism</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_Exploration" title="Age of Exploration">Age of Exploration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Traveling the ocean route allowed the Portuguese to avoid sailing across the highly disputed Mediterranean and traversing the dangerous <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>. The sum of the distances covered in the outward and return voyages made this expedition the longest ocean voyage ever made until then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>After decades of sailors trying to reach the Indies, with thousands of lives and dozens of vessels lost in shipwrecks and attacks, da Gama landed in <a href="/wiki/Kozhikode" title="Kozhikode">Calicut</a> on 20 May 1498. Unopposed access to the Indian <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice routes</a> boosted the economy of the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a>, which was previously based along northern and coastal West Africa. The main spices at first obtained from Southeast Asia were <a href="/wiki/Black_pepper" title="Black pepper">pepper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cinnamon" title="Cinnamon">cinnamon</a>, but soon included other products, all new to Europe. Portugal maintained a commercial monopoly of these commodities for several decades. It was not until a century later that other European powers, first the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a> and England, later France and Denmark, were able to challenge Portugal's monopoly and naval supremacy in the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Route" title="Cape Route">Cape Route</a>.
</p><p>Da Gama led two of the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_India_Armadas" title="Portuguese India Armadas">Portuguese India Armadas</a>, the first and the fourth. The latter was the largest and departed for India four years after his return from the first one. For his contributions, in 1524 da Gama was appointed <a href="/wiki/List_of_governors_of_Portuguese_India" title="List of governors of Portuguese India">Governor of India</a>, with the title of <a href="/wiki/Viceroy" title="Viceroy">Viceroy</a>, and was ennobled as <a href="/wiki/Count_of_Vidigueira" title="Count of Vidigueira">Count of Vidigueira</a> in 1519. He remains a leading figure in the history of exploration, and homages worldwide have celebrated his explorations and accomplishments. The Portuguese national epic poem, <i><a href="/wiki/Os_Lus%C3%ADadas" title="Os Lusíadas">Os Lusíadas</a></i>, was written in his honour by <a href="/wiki/Lu%C3%ADs_de_Cam%C3%B5es" title="Luís de Camões">Luís de Camões</a>.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sines06_edit1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Sines06_edit1.jpg/220px-Sines06_edit1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Sines06_edit1.jpg/330px-Sines06_edit1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Sines06_edit1.jpg/440px-Sines06_edit1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sines06_edit1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze statue of Vasco da Gama at his birthplace, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sines,_Portugal" title="Sines, Portugal">Sines, Portugal</a></div></div></div>
<p>Vasco da Gama was born in 1460 in the town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sines,_Portugal" title="Sines, Portugal">Sines</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3">[3]</a></sup> one of the few seaports on the <a href="/wiki/Alentejo" title="Alentejo">Alentejo</a> coast, southwest Portugal, probably in a house near the church of Nossa Senhora das Salas.
</p><p>Vasco da Gama's father was <a href="/wiki/Est%C3%AAv%C3%A3o_da_Gama_(15th_century)" title="Estêvão da Gama (15th century)">Estêvão da Gama</a>, who had served in the 1460s as a knight of the household of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infante_Ferdinand,_Duke_of_Viseu" title="Infante Ferdinand, Duke of Viseu">Infante Ferdinand, Duke of Viseu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ames_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ames-8">[8]</a></sup> He rose in the ranks of the military <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Order_of_Saint_James_of_the_Sword" title="Order of Saint James of the Sword">Order of Santiago</a>. Estêvão da Gama was appointed <i>alcaide-mór</i> (civil governor) of Sines in the 1460s, a post he held until 1478; after that he continued as a receiver of taxes and holder of the Order's commendas in the region.
</p><p>Estêvão da Gama married Isabel Sodré, a daughter of João Sodré (also known as João de Resende), scion of a well-connected family of English origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Her father and her brothers, <a href="/wiki/Vicente_Sodr%C3%A9" title="Vicente Sodré">Vicente Sodré</a> and Brás Sodré, had links to the household of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infante_Diogo,_Duke_of_Viseu" title="Infante Diogo, Duke of Viseu">Infante Diogo, Duke of Viseu</a>, and were prominent figures in the military <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Order_of_Christ_(Portugal)" title="Order of Christ (Portugal)">Order of Christ</a>. Vasco da Gama was the third of five sons of Estêvão da Gama and Isabel Sodré – in (probable) order of age: <a href="/wiki/Paulo_da_Gama" title="Paulo da Gama">Paulo da Gama</a>, João Sodré, Vasco da Gama, Pedro da Gama and Aires da Gama. Vasco also had one known sister, Teresa da Gama (who married Lopo Mendes de Vasconcelos).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Little is known of da Gama's early life. The Portuguese historian <a href="/wiki/Augusto_Carlos_Teixeira_de_Arag%C3%A3o" title="Augusto Carlos Teixeira de Aragão">Teixeira de Aragão</a> suggests that he studied at the inland town of <a href="/wiki/%C3%89vora" title="Évora">Évora</a>, which is where he may have learned mathematics and navigation. It has been claimed that he studied under <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Zacuto" title="Abraham Zacuto">Abraham Zacuto</a>, an astrologer and astronomer, but da Gama's biographer Subrahmanyam thinks this dubious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 1480, da Gama followed his father (rather than the Sodrés) and joined the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Order_of_Saint_James_of_the_Sword" title="Order of Saint James of the Sword">Order of Santiago</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The master of Santiago was Prince John, who ascended to the throne in 1481 as King <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Portugal" title="John II of Portugal">John II of Portugal</a>. John II doted on the Order, and the da Gamas' prospects rose accordingly.
</p><p>In 1492, John II dispatched da Gama on a mission to the port of <a href="/wiki/Set%C3%BAbal" title="Setúbal">Setúbal</a> and to the <a href="/wiki/Algarve" title="Algarve">Algarve</a> to seize <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">French</a> ships in retaliation for peacetime depredations against Portuguese shipping – a task that da Gama rapidly and effectively performed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration_before_da_Gama">Exploration before da Gama</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="1357" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png/300px-Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png/450px-Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png/600px-Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_discoveries" title="Portuguese discoveries">Portuguese discoveries</a> and explorations: first arrival places and dates</div></div></div>
<p>From the earlier part of the 15th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_discoveries" title="Portuguese discoveries">Portuguese expeditions</a> organized by Prince <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_the_Navigator" title="Henry the Navigator">Henry the Navigator</a> had been reaching down the African coastline, principally in search of west African riches (notably, gold and slaves).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> They had greatly extended Portuguese maritime knowledge, but had little profit to show for the effort. After Henry's death in 1460, the Portuguese Crown showed little interest in continuing this effort and, in 1469, licensed the neglected African enterprise to a private Lisbon merchant consortium led by <a href="/wiki/Fern%C3%A3o_Gomes" title="Fernão Gomes">Fernão Gomes</a>. Within a few years, Gomes' captains expanded Portuguese knowledge across the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Guinea" title="Gulf of Guinea">Gulf of Guinea</a>, doing business in gold dust, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Melegueta_pepper" title="Melegueta pepper">melegueta pepper</a>, ivory and <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">sub-Saharan slaves</a>. When Gomes' charter came up for renewal in 1474, Prince John (future John II), asked his father <a href="/wiki/Afonso_V_of_Portugal" title="Afonso V of Portugal">Afonso V of Portugal</a> to pass the African charter to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_partida_de_Vasco_da_Gama_para_a_%C3%8Dndia_em_1497.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="808" data-file-width="1224" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/A_partida_de_Vasco_da_Gama_para_a_%C3%8Dndia_em_1497.jpg/300px-A_partida_de_Vasco_da_Gama_para_a_%C3%8Dndia_em_1497.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/A_partida_de_Vasco_da_Gama_para_a_%C3%8Dndia_em_1497.jpg/450px-A_partida_de_Vasco_da_Gama_para_a_%C3%8Dndia_em_1497.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/A_partida_de_Vasco_da_Gama_para_a_%C3%8Dndia_em_1497.jpg/600px-A_partida_de_Vasco_da_Gama_para_a_%C3%8Dndia_em_1497.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_partida_de_Vasco_da_Gama_para_a_%C3%8Dndia_em_1497.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vasco da Gama leaving the port of <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a>, Portugal</div></div></div>
<p>Upon becoming king in 1481, <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Portugal" title="John II of Portugal">John II of Portugal</a> set out on many long reforms. To break the monarch's dependence on the feudal nobility, John II needed to build up the royal treasury; he considered royal commerce to be the key to achieving that. Under John II's watch, the gold and slave trade in west Africa was greatly expanded. He was eager to break into the highly profitable <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice trade</a> between Europe and Asia, which was conducted chiefly by land. At the time, this was virtually monopolized by the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a>, who operated overland routes via <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levantine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egyptian</a> ports, through the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> across to the spice markets of India. John II set a new objective for his captains: to find a sea route to Asia by sailing around the African continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scammell,_1981,_p._232_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scammell,_1981,_p._232-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the time Vasco da Gama was in his 20s, the king's plans were coming to fruition. In 1487, John II dispatched two spies, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pero_da_Covilh%C3%A3" title="Pero da Covilhã">Pero da Covilhã</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afonso_de_Paiva" title="Afonso de Paiva">Afonso de Paiva</a>, overland via Egypt to East Africa and India, to scout the details of the spice markets and trade routes. The breakthrough came soon after, when John II's captain <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeu_Dias" title="Bartolomeu Dias">Bartolomeu Dias</a> returned from rounding the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a> in 1488, having explored as far as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_River,_Eastern_Cape" title="Fish River, Eastern Cape">Fish River</a> (<i>Rio do Infante</i>) in modern-day South Africa and having verified that the unknown coast stretched away to the northeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scammell,_1981,_p._232_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Scammell,_1981,_p._232-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>An explorer was needed who could prove the link between the findings of Dias and those of da Covilhã and de Paiva, and connect these separate segments into a potentially lucrative trade route across the Indian Ocean.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="First_voyage">First voyage</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output
